0575081600|5.0|(Note: this review is of the 2007 Orion edition, updated and revised for the 25th anniversary of the release of the film)<br /><br />Paul M. Sammon's Future Noir: The Making of Blade Runner is not merely a must-have book for any fan of the film - it is _the_ must-have book, both for Blade Runner fans and for anyone interested in thorough and quite readable account of how a film comes to be made and the incredible number of things that influence the evolution of a film as it goes from original idea to finished product.<br /><br />Future Noir is clearly a labor of love for Sammon, who started following the project from its early inception and has continued to follow it through its evolution and ultimate release - and its later re-release and multiple revised cuts - and the long-term impact it has had on the scifi film genre. First published in 1996 - some fourteen years after the film's original release - he has gone back and updated it to coincide with the film's 25th anniversary and the release of Ridley Scott's Blade Runner: The Final Cut which is supposedly the director's ultimate and last word on the film. The revised edition of the book does in fact contain a great deal of new material which makes it worth finding and reading over the original edition.<br /><br />In reading Future Noir, one quickly becomes aware of just how thorough Sammon has been, having interviewed seemingly _everyone_ who had any involvement in the making of the film, from Philip K. Dick, the author of Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?, the novel on which Blade Runner was based, to Charles Lauzirika, the DVD producer who was instrumental not only in the production of the Special Edition DVD set for the Final Cut of the film but also in locating an enormous trove of \\"lost\\" film from the original production without which the Final Cut would never have happened. And in between of course are quotes from extensive interviews with director Ridley Scott; screenwriters Hampton Fancher and David Peoples; _all_ of the actors, from the major ones (Harrison Ford, Sean Young, Rutger Hauer, Darryl Hannah, Brion James, Joanna Cassidy, Edward James Olmos, William Sanderson, Joe Turkel) to the minor ones (M. Emmet Walsh, James Hong, Hy Pike, Morgan Paull and others), and dozens upon dozens of people involved in the more technical aspects of the film.<br /><br />Sammon's prose is both wonderfully descriptive and engaging, as can be seen in this bit on what makes Blade Runner so significant as a film:<br /><br />\\"Blade Runner presents one of the most elaborately visualized fictional environments ever constructed for an American film; each frame is bursting with an obsessive accretion of detail. Still, it's not a pretty sight. Ridley Scott's twenty-first century is a decayed, jaded, mutated place, a cheerless landscape whose meager humanity is being ground down by the microchipped jackboot of a ruthless technological zeitgeist. Its mean streets teem with hundreds of oddly dressed citizens (mostly Asians, some punks, street gangs, Hare Krishnas, and the ever-present police). All scurrying ratlike through concrete canyons whose confines are constantly bombarded by ubiquitous neon advertising, by the blare of unctuous announcers hawking pristine 'Off-world colonies,' from huge, insanely graphic-heavy blimps, and by the sodden, perpetual downpour of a numbing acid rain.<br />--It is exactly this deluge of details -- the striking costumes, the fantastic flying cars, the atmospheric ethnicity, the moody music, the lavish, lived-in sets -- which makes Blade Runner such addictive eye-candy. And it is to this bewitching visual surface which most viewers repeatedly return. Like its industrial counterparts in the worlds of high fashion and architecture, Blade Runner is a form of ultrasophisticated 'designer cinema', one whose astonishingly complex visual field has, despite a subsequent decade's worth of futuristic/alternate world spectaculars like Time Burton's Batman tilogy or Judge Dredd, remained the high-water mark against which all other big-buget SF entertainments are measured.\\"<br /><br />In addition to Sammon's narrative attention to detail, Future Noir is also lavishly illustrated with hundreds of production shots taken during the making of the film giving the reader an intimate feeling of what it must have been like to witness the making of Blade Runner.<br /><br />My only quibble with the book (at least the 2007 edition that I read) is that it lacks an index.  It's still a great read, but the lack of an index makes it hard to look up particular points of interest if you're trying to do research or answer a specific question.<br /><br />Highly, highly recommended for anyone with an interest in Blade Runner as a film or in how the film-making industry works in general.ainst which all other big-buget SF entertainments are measured.\\"    In addition to Sammon's narrative attention to detail, Future Noir is also lavishly illustrated with hundreds of production shots taken during the making of the film giving the reader an intimate feeling of what it must have been like to witness the making of Blade Runner.    My only quibble with the book (at least the 2007 edition that I read) is that it lacks an index.  It's still a great read, but the lack of an index makes it hard to look up particular points of interest if you're trying to do research or answer a specific question.    Highly, highly recommended for anyone with an interest in Blade Runner as a film or in how the film-making industry works in general.

(INNW), seems to complete the transition from the funky metal to the laid-back melodic band that we caught glimpses in songs like Drive, Stellar, and Mexico, and started to see coming into full force on songs like Dig and Love Hurts.    Ultimately, however, no matter what kind of an evolution a band is going through, the only thing that really matters to me is, do I like the songs? Do I want to listen to them on a regular basis? SCIENCE and Make Yourself were truly masterpieces - every song was good, and about half of the songs on Morning View were on that level of greatness. ACLOTM and Light Grenades were low points for the band, IMO, as only about 3 songs on each of those albums were worthwhile.    INNW falls into the middle of the spectrum, just like Morning View. About half of the songs are good or great - good enough that they'll stay on my iPod anyway. The other half are not good enough, and unless they really grow on me over the next few weeks of listening, I'll probably delete them from my iPod. 1402238843|4.0|Between 1921 and 1972, in addition to a number of mysteries and other less-remembered works, Heyer turned out nearly three dozen historical romances set in the Regency era -- the second decade of the 19th century, during the close of the generation-long war with France. Naturally, though none of them is terrible, some of them are rather better than others. I've read most of them over the years, and I put this delightfully complex story among the top three or four. Twenty-five-year-old Venetia Lanyon is mistress of a comfortable though not huge estate a dozen miles from York, managing the place while her brother, Conway, serves in his regiment during the Allied occupation of France -- both their parents being dead. In fact, she's been expecting him to sell out and come home for some time now, but he apparently prefers the life of a gentleman officer. She shares the house with her sixteen-year-old brother, Aubrey, who suffers from a bad hip and a short leg and who loathes pity and being helped; he's also a promising scholar preparing to leave for Cambridge, so Venetia isn't worried about him. She's never been to London, never \\"come out\\" in society, and is on the way to spinsterhood, but she's of a very independent turn of mind and those trivialities don't much bother her either. Of course, she's a stunning beauty and at least two respectable local guys have designs on her -- one rather smarmy and superior prig and one six years her junior -- and Venetia is frequently annoyed that neither they nor anyone else seems to believe that she really means whatever she says. So the scene has been set, . . . and then things get interesting when Baron Damerel, a noted rake, turns up at the Priory, his estate adjacent to the Lanyons. Damerel is a dozen years older than Venetia but his reputation as a rake was established in his youth when he eloped to the Continent with an older, married lady. And he seems to have made every effort to live up to his bad press since. Damerel and Venetia, both being not much concerned with other people's opinions, quickly become close friends, to the scandal of friends and family. The Baron, of course, isn't so vile as he's made out to be and friendship gradually turns to something else. The plot develops gradually and Heyer tosses in a couple of very nicely crafted narrative bombshells later on -- the nature of which I decline to divulge. Not only the half-dozen major characters but a good many minor supporting players are carefully portrayed in vivid and multiple dimensions and the dialogue is some of Heyer's sparkling best. Best of all, it's not all just frothy fantasy but an interesting picture of a intelligent, capable young woman's quest to obtain what she discovers she really wants out of life.netia, both being not much concerned with other people's opinions, quickly become close friends, to the scandal of friends and family. The biocentric universe may be a transformational resolution of the current crisis in physics -- but the way Lanza presents the theory, it's unlikely to make any inroads. He should have stuck close to the physics and laid out the program in a linear manner, guiding the reader through the complete mental paradigm shift necessary to grasp it. Instead, he takes a popular \\"new age\\" approach, telling endless personal anecdotes, speaking of things like a \\"field of mind\\" (meaningless), and relying heavily on the vague idea of \\"consciousness\\" to explain all -- without even examining what consciousness is. No one calls it the \\"two hole experiment.\\") Worse, Lanza's appearances with Deepak Chopra are disastrous for having this theory taken seriously. But if you're interested in the science that grounds biocentric theory, you will need to look beyond Lanza's writings. The Wikipedia article on biocentrism has some good links, including the physics-oriented Biocentricity website, which explains how these ideas are not as mystical as Lanza would have you believe. B004WKOCP8|3.0|On its seventh studio release [[ASIN:B004WKOCP8 If Not Now, When?]], rock band Incubus are noticeably more reserved than they have been in the past.  The album's restrained nature is prone to off-put fans who are used to the more aggressive sounds that characterize the band, even on 2006's [[ASIN:B000JBXXZ4 Light Grenades (Snys)]] with cuts like the passionate \\"Dig\\" or the driving \\"Anna Molly\\" and \\"A Kiss To Send Us Off.\\" This newfound restraint does not make this album `bad,' but it does restrain some of the excitability of previous, more dynamic work.<br /><br />Title cut and opener \\"If Not Now, When?\\" is the first sign of a more restrained Incubus.  Opening with a synthetic string pad (sort of), the cut eventually settles into a definitive groove, though it is very simple and minimalist with Brandon Boyd's lead vocals serving as the most exciting feature of the cut.  While \\"If Not Now, When?\\" never reaches the dynamic heights of other Incubus cuts, Boyd does sound as great as ever and the cut itself grows on one with successive listens, even if it is never a `hit' per say. The bridge is a highlight.  \\"Promises, Promises\\" is a bit more `moving' than its preceding cut; a quicker tempo contrasts the slow tempo of \\"If Now Now, When?\\" \\"Promises, Promises\\" remains restrained, particularly given the prominent use of keyboard, however the melody is notable and the songwriting and production are also high points.  Some will still yearn that the solid \\"Promises, Promises\\" could be even more `soul stirring,' but it is enjoyable and nothing for Incubus to hang their head down about.<br /><br />\\"Friends And Lovers\\" opens mysteriously, but a solid groove is established.  Like the previous cuts, \\"Friends and Lovers\\" never feels `urgent,' meaning everything kind of sits and simmers but never reaches a `boiling point.'  Pleasant, the track is not necessarily memorable by any means.  \\"Thieves\\" hones in on minimalism, which is a pro. The lyricism of the cut might be its selling point, dealing with politically- and socially-charged themes.  The build-up of \\"Thieves\\" is more considerable than the previous three, but still there is a lack of that extra `something.' \\"Isadore\\" is a `B' cut at best coming off as the most forgettable cut at this point.  The main rub with \\"Isadore\\" is the vibe given off is just `too sleepy,' even given Incubus's `low key' vibe across this effort.  The chorus is effective: \\"If not who you were/it's who you are/and we've come this far, lover/No I won't rest until the world/knows the name Isadore.\\"<br /><br />\\"The Original\\" falls somewhat in the same trap of \\"Isadore\\" until it reaches an epic 3:44 mark in which the band achieves a bit more sound, depth, and grit; the first three-plus minutes proved bland and uninspiring.  The guitar effects nearing the close of this five-minute cut are sound and the chorus is catchy if nothing else: \\"Doors were starting to close/and you drew a rose/my,my/your mind it is original/yeah, you're a skeleton key/opening me/my,my/your mind it is original/Girl you're the original/always and always will be...\\" What is appreciated is the sincerity that Incubus try to attain, though more acceleration of intensity and grit would have made \\"Isadore\\" more well-rounded.  \\"Defiance\\" falls short despite its faster tempo while \\"In The Company of Wolves\\" is only slightly more sound, given its change of style within it's seven-minute plus duration.<br />\\"Switchblade\\" redirects conceded momentum by the boggy \\"Defiance\\" and \\"In the Company of Wolves\\" finding the band sounding much more energized like their past work.  The quasi-rap/sung performance by Boyd is perfect for this cut and the songwriting is superb. The highlight is definitely the well-penned refrain: \\"Nobody wears it like you anymore/Nobody wears it like you anymore/Light as a feather/bright like a dying star/cut those together/girl that is what you are/Wild eyes forever/built to punish and persuade/I'd like to get here/but all I got was a switchblade.\\" \\"Adolescents,\\" the first single from If Not Now, When? is enjoyable, finding the band experimenting with mixed-meter and finding Boyd achieving more vigor within his vocals.  Closing cut \\"Tomorrow's Food\\" ends the album less exceptionally than it began, but is not a bad showing by any means.  Boyd's lead vocals are commanding and the groove has a folksy edge to it, somewhat reminiscent of the 1960s... sort of.<br /><br />Essentially, [[ASIN:B004WKOCP8 If Not Now, When?]] is not a bad album, even proving enjoyable within its best moments.  The effect, however, is that the band try to `reach for the stars' on this effort but never attain this; some of the experiments fly high while others fail to resound with such glory. The best cuts? - \\"If Not Now, When?,\\" \\"Promises, Promises,\\" \\"Switchblade,\\" and \\"Adolescents.\\"the first single from If Not Now, When? is enjoyable, finding the band experimenting with mixed-meter and finding Boyd achieving more vigor within his vocals. That just because they can't stop screwing anyone that walks by, other cultures don't and families actually look alike? If you have read my Top 20 animated list, you'll see how often his name appears. Studio Ghibli has been responsible for many good films, but Nausica&auml; of the Valley of the Wind was actually released in 1984, just before the studio was founded. It remains Miyazaki's most ambitious work because of its epic scope.<br /><br />Nausica&auml; is set approximately a thousand years in the future when pollution levels have threatened to destroy life on the planet. The land is dominated by the Toxic Jungle which is filled with poisonous plants. The jungle is protected by giant insects and other creatures.<br /><br />We meet Nausica&auml; (Lohman) early in the film when she discovers a discarded ohm shell. Ohms are giant creatures which seems wiser than any humans they may encounter. Nausica&auml; recovers one of the parts of the shell and takes it home. Ohms are not always calm and their eyes grow red with rage when they are angry. Nausica&auml; helps save Lord Yupa (Stewart), a master swordsman, from an enraged ohm.<br /><br />Yupa knows Nausica&auml; well and has a present for her; a small fox squirrel which she names Teto. Her first encounter with the creature shows us her true nature. She says that there is nothing to fear, but the fox-squirrel bites her. She makes no move, but simply repeats that there is nothing to fear. It stops biting and licks the wound. It's such a touching scene and gives a hint at how Nausica&auml; interacts with strangers later in the story. She's an easy character to love.<br /><br />Nausica&auml;'s life is peaceful. She lives in the Valley of the Winds where everyone works together in harmony farming the land. Although her father is the king, princess Nausica&auml; doesn't put herself above other people. She lends a hand repairing machinery or whatever else is needed. Her people love her; especially the children. For any parent thinking of showing the film to their children, Nausica&auml; is a good role model.<br /><br />The film has quite a few battle sequences, but they are brief and involve misguided people who think their causes are just. I think Miyazaki is showing us what could happen if we continue to pollute and exploit the planet without giving any thought to the future. It's a common theme in his stories and is more prominent here than in later films.<br /><br />Unlike any other animated film I have seen, Nausica&auml; of the Valley of the Wind shows us a vast world. We explore some of it and see Nausica&auml; discover secrets about the world and the creatures inhabiting it. She has a way with animals and insects and seeks solutions that avoid killing any kind of creature. She seems to empathize and realize how to stop seemingly wild creatures from attacking. People around her are frequently amazed by her actions.<br /><br />The film shows the futility of war and the power people have to change their lives by thinking about their course of action. So much happens in the two hour running time that the film seems to move at a breakneck pace. There is always something happening, whether it's action or a discovery of some kind.<br /><br />Joe Hisaishi is again responsible for the music, and it's one of the best scores he has ever produced. There's a particular scene with a piece of music usingchildren's voices which has me in tears every time. I'm not sure why, but the music is powerful and fits the scenes perfectly.<br /><br />I know I haven't revealed much of the story. That's because I want you to discover the secrets for yourself. Nausica&auml; of the Valley of the Wind reminds me of scenes in Avatar and Star Wars, but the story is arguably more powerful than both. If you have seen other titles from Hayao Miyazaki, be aware that this contains more adult themes than most. That said, it can and should be enjoyed by the whole family.<br /><br />Video Quality 4.5/5<br />If you have seen any of Miyazaki's films, you'll know that his animation style looks nothing like modern studios such as Pixar or Dreamworks. He's an artist in the true sense of the word and the frames of the films look like watercolor paintings. It's been 27 years since the film was released, so the animation style looks a little dated. Some of the supporting characters in crowds won't move, but the overall effect is still wonderful. Disney has delivered another great transfer. Colors improve dramatically over the DVD version. Some scenes look slightly soft, but that's partly due to the animation style. This doesn't look like Up, Ratatouille or Rango, but detail is strong and any Miyazaki fan will be delighted with the result.<br /><br />Audio Quality 4/5<br />The film comes with three audio mixes. Disney failed to provide a lossless Japanese option for Ponyo, but purists will be happy to see the Japanese: DTS-HD Master Audio 2.0 track included this time. Other versions include English and French: DTS-HD Master Audio 2.0. Although a 5.1 mix would have been welcome, I'm not disappointed by the options on offer. Dialogue is clear throughout, while battle scenes pack a considerable punch. Ambient sounds such as wind in the valley come across well, as does Joe Hisaishi's score.<br /><br />Special Features 3/5<br /><br />The additional features are split between the BD and the DVD.<br /><br />As with other Miyazaki films, Nausica&auml; of the Valley of the Wind includes the option to view the entire film with the original Japanese storyboards. It's interesting to see how Miyazaki's original sketches developed.<br /><br />Enter the Lands of Ghibli gives the viewer the option to click on characters from some of the other films. There's not a huge amount of content, but it's nice to see.<br /><br />Behind the Studio: Creating Nausica&auml; (12 minutes, HD) - A brief feature which includes thoughts from Miyazaki.<br /><br />The Birth Story of Studio Ghibli (28 minutes, SD) - A TV documentary from Japan talking about Studio Ghibli's origins.<br /><br />Behind the Microphone (8 minutes, SD) - The American cast is shown recording some of the scenes and talking about the film.<br /><br />Original Japanese Trailers and TV Spots (8 minutes, SD)<br /><br />Nausica&auml; of the Valley of the Wind includes a lot of themes and elements that we have come to expect in a Hayao Miyazaki movie, but it's more epic in scope. Like Avatar, this makes me feel like I am stepping onto another world. Unlike Avatar, the dialogue isn't dumb in any way. I like Nausica&auml; as a character because her intentions are always good. She sees the best in everyone and is a positive force. The whole experience makes the film one I love to revisit and it's always rated among my favorite animated titles. Disney's Blu-ray presentation does the film justice and is highly recommended as a story that can be enjoyed by the whole family.<br /><br />Overall score 4.5/5e secrets for yourself. Nausica of the Valley of the Wind reminds me of scenes in Avatar and Star Wars, but the story is arguably more powerful than both. If you have seen other titles from Hayao Miyazaki, be aware that this contains more adult themes than most. That said, it can and should be enjoyed by the whole family.    Video Quality 4.5/5  If you have seen any of Miyazaki's films, you'll know that his animation style looks nothing like modern studios such as Pixar or Dreamworks. He's an artist in the true sense of the word and the frames of the films look like watercolor paintings. It's been 27 years since the film was released, so the animation style looks a little dated. Some of the supporting characters in crowds won't move, but the overall effect is still wonderful. Disney has delivered another great transfer. Colors improve dramatically over the DVD version. Some scenes look slightly soft, but that's partly due to the animation style. This doesn't look like Up, Ratatouille or Rango, but detail is strong and any Miyazaki fan will be delighted with the result.    Audio Quality 4/5  The film comes with three audio mixes. Disney failed to provide a lossless Japanese option for Ponyo, but purists will be happy to see the Japanese: DTS-HD Master Audio 2.0 track included this time. Other versions include English and French: DTS-HD Master Audio 2.0. Although a 5.1 mix would have been welcome, I'm not disappointed by the options on offer. Dialogue is clear throughout, while battle scenes pack a considerable punch. Ambient sounds such as wind in the valley come across well, as does Joe Hisaishi's score. As with other Miyazaki films, Nausica of the Valley of the Wind includes the option to view the entire film with the original Japanese storyboards. It's interesting to see how Miyazaki's original sketches developed.    Enter the Lands of Ghibli gives the viewer the optionto click on characters from some of the other films. Behind the Microphone (8 minutes, SD) - The American cast is shown recording some of the scenes and talking about the film.    Original Japanese Trailers and TV Spots (8 minutes, SD)    Nausica of the Valley of the Wind includes a lot of themes and elements that we have come to expect in a Hayao Miyazaki movie, but it's more epic in scope. Like Avatar, this makes me feel like I am stepping onto another world. Unlike Avatar, the dialogue isn't dumb in any way. I like Nausica as a character because her intentions are always good. She sees the best in everyone and is a positive force. The whole experience makes the film one I love to revisit and it's always rated among my favorite animated titles. B0053EZXRS|4.0|Not intended for the timid artistic souls amongst us.<br /><br />Falling into and under the \\"grindhouse\\" genre of filmmaking, this is the best of Saturday-Night-At-The-Drive-In homage: Naked women, extreme bloody violence and outrageous storytelling. I remember this period of movies well and have very fond memories of places that today exist only in historical images and in the hearts of wistful times long gone. These action/thrillers and ubiquitous kung-fu flicks were the dominant form of cheap entertainment from the late 1960s through the early 1980s when home video cassettes were introduced into the marketplace, thus killing the popular weekend gatherings. It's actually quite sad. I can recall families and friends who would meet up at the drive-in, bringing their potluck foodstuffs, kids all running around, having a blast as a community at the drive-in; now we're separated by byte and HD home set-ups. (apologies for the misty tangent)<br /><br />It's interesting how much more enjoyable Cage can be when he actually acts, and by that I mean, when he stops acting like himself. Because his real personality is so much less enjoyable, less interesting and infinitely more lame. Pompous overbearing fool would be a step up if you've ever encountered the man. So I really enjoy the times when he's able to put aside his real-life persona in favor of a movie character. And playing Milton (obvious nod to 'Paradise Lost' author of the same moniker) has Cage at his best - his real self is completely gone and replaced by a seriously focused deceased dude on a hellbent mission.<br /><br />Shotgun blast of pure adrenaline with Amber Heard portraying kick-ass temptress Piper, lending her ample assets to help Milton save his granddaughter from a demented cult. Her performance is spot-on perfect. With just enough trailer trash, just enough action, just enough hot pants, and just enough modern fully-realized hip chick to pull off her role with supreme skill. Impressed with this young actress; it'll be interesting to see what she does next.<br /><br />And Hell hath no fury like The Accountant; the Devil's own bean-counter who doesn't take it well when one of the beans jumps out of the pot. He's a consummate actor of range who always seems to be on the verge of breaking into big screen leading man roles, but unfortunately might be too gothic to pull off the all important romantic lead. Which is just as well, I'm fond of his cool calculating characters.<br /><br />Loved the extreme explosions, outstanding muscle car chases and the over-the-top brutal violence. 0385333846|5.0|I first read Kurt Vonnegut's Slaughterhouse-Five as a very young man.  At the time, I was an avid fan of science fiction, especially those books featuring time travel, and that is what drew me to Slaughterhouse-Five.  As a science fiction novel, I thought it was pretty good, especially considering the amount of random time travel its main character, Billy Pilgrim, experiences in this relatively short novel.  The problem with my early assessment of Slaughterhouse-Five, however, is that this is not simply a science fiction novel - and it should not be judged by the standards of that genre.  Even worse, I managed to ignore the novel's message.<br /><br />Vonnegut's deceptively simple masterpiece is about life itself; it is about the futility and utter waste of warfare; it is about time, and the way that we perceive it; it is about fate and whether any of us really has any control over what happens to us next.  Poor Billy Pilgrim certainly had little to say about the course of his own life.  Swept up into World War II, where he is captured by the Germans almost as soon as he arrives, Billy will be held prisoner in Dresden's Slaughterhouse-Five, from where he will survive the Allied firebombing that destroys the entire city.  He will be abducted by a crew of aliens from the planet Tralfamador and displayed in a zoo there along with the former porn star chosen as his mate.  He will become a successful optometrist, popular and respected in his community.  The only problem is that it all happens at the same time.<br /><br />Billy Pilgrim has become \\"unstuck in time\\" and he never knows, from one instant to the next, when he will flash forward or backward to a different part of his lifetime.  It is all real, and it is always happening - all of it at the same time.<br /><br />Slaughterhouse-Five is generally considered to be a classic anti-war novel.  Even with that reputation, its message is subtle enough that it is possible to get so caught up in the rest of the story and its mechanics that the novel's serious theme is only recognized some time after turning its final page.  This book is funny, even to the point of being absurd, but it is a serious piece of writing by an author with something serious to say about the foolishness of killing \\"enemies\\" by the thousands/millions at the behest of politicians who have failed at their own jobs.<br /><br />The effectiveness of Slaughterhouse-Five is compounded by the ease with which it can be read; Vonnegut has disguised a complex novel, one filled with thoughtful points, as some kind of comedic science fiction piece.  And, he makes it all look so easy.el's serious theme is only recognized some time after turning its final page. The effectiveness of Slaughterhouse-Five is compounded by the ease with which it can be read; Vonnegut has disguised a complex novel, one filled with thoughtful points, as some kind of comedic science fiction piece.  And, he makes it all look so easy.

1400052181|5.0|Finished this book within 24 hours of starting it. Highly recommended.    HeLa cells are famous in biology for their almost unparalleled ability to survive and replicate. The cells are responsible for numerous breakthroughs including medical techniques, testing the polio vaccine, AIDS research, and many more. The book looks at the life of Henrietta Lacks and her family through ethical, racial, and political lenses. The great irony is the multi-billion dollar industry and immeasurable combined success of numerous people (scientists, doctors, Nobel prize winners) yet her family is unable to afford healthcare.    If you're a biology student, you must read this book. Even if you're not (like me), you can appreciate the story because we have all benefited from research involving the HeLa cell line. The issues raised in the book are well-researched, accessible, and important to modern society. The questions raised about who should profit from individual tissue samples, the difficulties of patenting cell lines, and how racism played and plays roles in medical and modern society were provoking. This is the sort of book you read and immediately give to someone saying \\"you have to read this book!\\" It's a book I want to discuss with others and one that made me think. I can't recommend it highly enough.

So to speak. Otherwise the action sequences are out of place. I'll explain my point.     What we see in the opening sequence is Baby Doll, a person who even this early in life has gone through some kind of traumatic experience with her step-father. What we see, in hurried sequence, is how a person remembers how they got to where they are now. We see the implications of a sexually abusive relationship and we see the terrible death of Baby Doll's sister. She is eventually shunted into an awful asylum which is implied to be a place where they sent \\"uncooperative\\" women. In five days, our main character will be lobotomized. Based on what she sees, she concocts an escape plan. But what happens in those five days is horrific---too traumatic for Baby Doll to take. She therefore constructs several layers of fantasy. The second, the \\"bordello\\" layer, is a metaphor for Baby Doll's perception of the asylum. Note her PERCEPTION: we see later that things aren't always as they appear. It should also be noted that the character performances are simplified in the same way that you cannot know the inner lives of your closest friends. You are you, and they are in your head as you see them: mere grasps of the complex entity that is themselves.     If you don't know what the second layer's supposed to be and how it connects, well....how many fantasies have you HAD in your life? o.O;; The second layer is Baby Doll's fantasy reflection on what occurred: a protection that many people create to escape the stress of their lives. Baby Doll's life is simply more stressful than we can imagine. In a woman's fantasies, she often sexualizes herself out of a desire to be \\"better\\" as it were. I know this from experience; I did it even as a thirteen year old girl. XD We are also however impossibly powerful, gloriously badass, fighting nameless enemies as a representation of our very real fears and resentments. It's true that this is an odd thing to watch, deeply private for most of us, but if you know what it is it becomes rather more dramatic. ;3 She's actually astonishingly unselfish: how many of your friends take central roles in your private world?     In short it was a blast: the true villains were painful to see (There ARE real people like Blue in the world, and I was proud of the movie for being honest about the dark subject matter in its own bizarre way) and the victory was of a different kind. But in a world where you have so little power, a happy ending would have been deeply unrealistic. The movie shows you a very real hell some people live in and it shows the kind of victory you may be lucky enough to find there. A victory in which you win by being stronger psychologically than those who crush you underneath them. Save another's life, perhaps.     So in short...I doubt it's for everyone. The themes are deeply disturbing to most people, when they're thought about. It creates a sickening feeling in the chest, the proverbial sucker punch. Intolerable to some. The dialogue is a little crazy at points and sometimes the story wandered more than it should, focused on the wrong elements. But I saw no misogynistic tendencies in it: merely the revealing of what real misogyny is like to the powerless. So...make of it what you will. It is a strange movie.

B004UHF51S|5.0|I've been an admirer of Michael Franks' records and the songs within for decades now, and only discovered him because of the openness of FM radio in the 70s.  A station such as WNEW-FM, known for it's rock sensibility, frequently played tunes off \\"The Art of Tea\\" and even interviewed him in studio one morning.  I was immediately hooked (and floored) by the intelligent lyrics even before I could begin to sit back and let those melodies sink in. No one could drop cultural and historical references in his songs with such aplomb while dressing them in classy, palatable jazz arrangements.  His persona at the time seemed to be that of a more than casual observer and participant in the trials and tribulations of love and romance.  Over the years, those cynical views had largely given way to a picture of domesticated bliss, while taking a sideways glance at the past. Just one look at the cover art of \\"Time Together\\", and the first thing a seasoned fan might notice is the absence of tropical imagery, showing instead a pastoral setting depicting him, his wife, and their recently departed, beloved dog. Franks' latest release, \\"Time Together\\", appears to have completed some kind of musical and lyrical circle.  The relatively spare arrangements are reminiscent of Franks' earliest releases, though his lyrics are now reflections of someone who is both looking back at his life so far with an appreciative eye, and forward with the wonder of a child.  If it's possible, his lyrics reveal an almost \\"mature innocence\\".  This is someone who knows the meaning of life.  The usual all-star roster of sidemen makes a distinguished appearance, and in particular, I was happy to see brilliant pianist and producer Gil Goldstein (who played in my favorite touring band of Michael's way back when) lend a steady hand.  Franks is one of those unique songwriters who can hold your attention with his words, even if the band takes a 2 or 3 minute solo. The artist seems to be extending a hand to his audience this time, inviting it in to share his world. This is a lovely addition to Franks' body of work, and an album I can easily recommend to longtime fans, and those who need an introduction.

B004SIPA4G|5.0|No question I am a film noir, especially a crime film noir, aficionado. Recently I have been on a tear reviewing various <em>crime noir</em> efforts and drawing comparisons between the ones that \\"speak\\" to me and those that, perhaps, should have been better left on the cutting room floor. The classics are easy and need no additional comment from me as their plot lines stand on their own merits. Others, because they have a fetching, or wicked, for that matter, <em>femme fatale</em> to muddy the waters also get a pass. Of course when I think of noir it is 1940s-50s noir, black and white in film and in the good guys-bad guys constellation with a little murder and mayhem mixed in to keep one's eyes open just in case there is no <em>femme fatale </em>to muddy the waters. Neo-noir, such as the film under review, Sin City, is another matter, perhaps. Here's the why of the perhaps.    Central to the old time <em>crime noir</em> was the notion that crime did not pay and as stated above the bad guy(s) learned that lesson the hard way after a little mussing up or a date with a bullet. Kids' stuff really when compared to the over-the-top action of this three vignettes series on modern day good guys versus bad guys. Three separate male characters, all tough guys and guys you would want to have at your back if real trouble headed your way, are trying, trying within the parameters of common sense or believability, to clean up slices of Sin City. Sin City as the rather obvious name implies, is in the grips of corruption from the top down, including in virtually every civic institution. Our avengers are trying to cut a wedge into that bad karma by individually, one, tracking down a bizarre, politically connected heir whose thing was slice and dice of very young girls, two, avenge the death of a high class call girl who was kind to one tough guy, and, three, keep the pimps and cops at bay in the red light district where the working girls have set up their own Hookers' Commune.     All of this doing good is, of necessity in today's movie world, linked up with, frankly, over the top use of violence of all sorts from cannibalism to barbaric death sentences, well beyond what tame old time noir warranted. Apparently the succeeding crime waves since the 1940s have upped the ante and something like total war is required to exterminate the villains. That and some very up-to-date use of cinematography to give a gritty black and white feel to the adventures. And also a not small dose of magical realism, suspension of disbelief, and sparseness of language to go along with the plot and visual action.     But here is the funny thing, funny for an old-time crime noir aficionado, I really liked this film. Why? Well go back to the old time crime noir premise. Good guys (and then it was mostly guys- here some very wicked \\"dames\\" join in and I know I would not want to cross them, no way) pushed their weight around or tilted at windmills for cheap dough or maybe a little kiss. They got mussed, up, trussed up, busted up in the cause of some individual justice drive that drove the \\"better angels of their natures.\\" Guess what, sixty years later, a thousand years advanced cinematically, a million years advanced socially (maybe) and these guys are still chasing windmills. Nice, right.

The studio goes through the motions, which makes for a more-than-decent facsimile, but in the end it doesn't feel as complete and engaging as their other masterworks. I would've loved it as a kid, and plan to share it with mine some day, but as an adult I'd rather throw in Toy Story or The Incredibles.

This is the first on body language that I have read and it is a good introduction. Even though it is a short read and written on the popular level, I can already read others much better than before. More importantly, I am more aware of my own body language and what I am communicating with it.<br /><br />After reading this, I am convinced that everyone should read at least one book on body language. If nothing else this novel blew the lid off the scope I thought I had on this world, giving me a greater admiration for Martin's vision and ability to bring that to his audience with artistry. It is presented in the middle of what I've seen a few other films do: some films simply vilify the wealthy men and show them getting their just desserts of financial collapse. Others push for the mythical view of chivalrous wealth.<br /><br />The Company Men tries to shoot down the middle. One character is troubled by the damage caused to attain his wealth, but his solution is hardly self-sacrificing. There's the one who can't cope, there's the one who just doesn't care, and the one who survives because he's got a great wife. What else? Of course, the hard-working folks who aren't living in the upper-class lifestyle who go back and forth between sympathy and near-gloating.<br /><br />In all, the mainline of this film is that life at the top may be well-compensated but it's still empty. It's easy to lob stones up the line, that with all that money they should have been happy and all that, to think how much better off they were than others, but the truth is, the crisis is just as real. The bottom still hurts, however far the fall.<br /><br />The movie had some nearly funny moments: if the depiction of \\"outplacement\\" hadn't been so close to the corporate self-help nonsense I've seen in my own life, it would have been hilarious. The main character, Bobby Walker (Ben Affleck) is this family man -- while we see shadows and the toils of tribulations of other higher level corporate types (Chris Cooper and Tommy Lee Jones.) At this point, you realize you're pretty much seeing microcosm of the 2010 financial crisis in the United States -- and that corporations are out to increases profits as much as possible, perhaps without the regard of the status of (formerly) successful employees. If you've been reading the news headlines of the past few years, the plot is quite predictable.    The middle of the film stagnates a bit -- we quickly know that Bobby is not going to find another similar position in the corporate world, but does find another opportunity in a vastly different industry. While embracing this new lifestyle, Bobby re-evaluates the world around him and finally takes stock in the change around him. Some of the other characters have a much harder time doing so, and there are significant consequences for them and their families -- reaching the denouement of the film. Craig T. Nelson's character, as the main antagonist, plays a good corporate president and shows little remorse towards those let go.    The ending is formulaic yet provides limited closure. Nonetheless, at the time I write this review the 2010 economic crisis still isn't resolved and hangs in the balance, much like this movie's conclusion. I've seen worse dramas and I've seen better -- the acting and casting is above average, it's just the story line that needs work, even though I didn't expect a happy ending.

0596805527|4.0|This text, in all of its editions, has maintained a valuable spot on my bookshelf for over a decade.  It's also the text I assign my students in an advanced client side scripting course I teach.  Obviously, I like this book, and I have a whole lot of respect for the author, who does a great job at explaining the language, particularly the many often misunderstood parts, in unparalleled detail and clarity.  There's no questioning the high and incomparable value of information contained inside.  It's famous and truly indispensable among us client-side coders, and therefore, I can give it no less than 4 well deserved stars despite the limitations which follow.   That said, it's not perfect and not a truly comprehensive source of information as other reviewers represent it as being.  There are holes in this text that will need to be filled from other sources.  Probably by design rather than oversight, Flanagan falls short on best practice information.  As Javascript matured, editions of this book has kept up with new features but they fail to keep up with the changes in the way we use it.  Common non-intuitive practices have been established to accommodate large scale web applications which are now common and these practices are not included in the book.  I consider this an unfortunate omission and necessitates companion book(s) to get the full picture (such as Stoyan Stefanov's Javascript Design Patterns and Douglas Crockford's Javascript the Good Parts).  With regards to the newly added server-side section, I think Flanagan would have been better off focusing completely on client-side as opposed to trying to squeeze in Node as well.  Looking ahead, I don't see this all-in-one approach being feasible for future editions as Node continues to grow in breadth and complexity... it will just be too much for a single book.  Even now, while I love the eventsource and websocket server-side demos, they were just too brief and didn't really demonstrate practical use of Node.js for real multi-page websites or come close to the comprehensive detail given to the client side.  The examples worked fine and demonstrated how you could quickly hack a small project together, but that's about it.  It wouldn't have taken much space to include information about Content Delivery Networks, gziping, minification, layering, and build tools, so I'm surprised this was left out.  These concepts, especially as libraries and modules are integrated from third parties, are absolutely critical to performance of modern web applications.  There are also some smaller strange omissions that are hardly worth mentioning... I'm only doing so hoping they will be read and noticed + included in the next editions:  - Regular expression chapter has no examples of positive and negative lookaheads.  This is frustrating considering these are usually the most difficult features to grasp.  - No xpath? (document.evaluate). Seems like this alternative should at least be mentioned along with Selectors API given how widely it's implimented.  - I get why Flanagan chose JQuery as the Javascript library to go over in this book--it's the most popular library out there.  By the way it was decoupled from the rest of the book, I assume its inclusion was either a last minute decision or one that might be corrected in the future if its popularity falters.  Like server side content however, I just feel this book is an odd place for this information.  Especially given the number of other high-quality library out there that aren't primarily for DOM manipulation (i.e. are relevant beyond the browser).  Other libraries are much better suited for certain tasks such as class based design (dojo, mootools, yui), utilizing future ecmascript features today (mootools), integrated build tools, etc.  - Personally, I don't find the last 300+ pages of reference material in the back of the book useful at all. This information would be much better presented online in an indexed+search, and I'd be happy to seeit removed/replaced in future editions as well. Save the paper!

B000002LAB|4.0|`Tutu', released in 1986 was one of Miles' last works and defines his later, `electronic' period. Sometimes criticised for being more the work of the highly talented multi-instrumentalist Marcus Miller than of Miles himself in the same way that `Porgy and Bess' and `Sketches of Spain' might be seen as `Gil Evans Albums', nevertheless Miles' signature style and virtuoso trumpet playing define Tutu's principle character.<br /><br />The history of this project is that Miles' new contract with Warner Brothers stipulated that all songwriting credits henceforth should belong to WB rather than to Miles himself. To get around this legal irritant Miller was hired to compose the main theme for each piece as a framework over which Miles could solo, and took all legal songwriting credit. Miller has been described as \\"A producer who could play as well as a player who could produce\\" and did a good job: the resulting electronic rhythms and synth-dominant sound gives `Tutu' a distinctively 1980s feel which leads some to claim that Miles had `re-invented' jazz for the fourth or fifth time in his illustrious and multiply-groundbeaking career of continuous innovation. Well, that's stretching things a bit: `Tutu' is OK but hardly `great', in the same way `Do-Bop' is OK but not really a classic.<br /><br />`Tutu' was certainly a new, fresh sound in 1986 and unlike anything else at the time which could be found under the `jazz' category. The album has a lot of energy and Miles' trumpet playing is as inventive and energetic as ever. However after several numbers it can begin to sound samey, and in the context of Davis' long career and particularly high-points such as `Kind of Blue,' `In a Silent Way' and `Bitches Brew', `Tutu' is justifiably seen by most (but not all) fans as a second-tier effort and not really one of his best works. The album is, however, easily accessible to the new-to-jazz listener and even to people who profess to `not like jazz' but are OK with high-energy electronic instrumental music, and so offers a gateway to the deeper and more interesting musical milestones of the genre.<br /><br />RIP Miles. You never failed to surprise and delight us, even with your more hit-and-miss projects like `Tutu.'instrumental music, and so offers a gateway to the deeper and more interesting musical milestones of the genre.<br /><br />RIP Miles. You never failed to surprise and delight us, even with your more hit-and-miss projects like `Tutu.'

Part of me wants to say that even a bad Incubus album is better than something other than Incubus, but I kind of doubt that after the newness of this album wears off I'll ever listen to it.  As it is I've had it in my CD player for a week, and haven't really developed any more appreciation for it than I had on my initial disappointing listen.     It isn't that this album is mellow.  I'm not one of those fans harping about \\"the old days\\" of Incubus yore.  I actually prefer some of their mellower songs like \\"Are you In?\\" \\"Oil and Water\\" \\"Drive\\" and even most of the Crow Left of the Murder album.  But this album is just lacking that vibe and flare that Incubus is known for.  You can still have 'teeth' and sing mellow music as they've demonstrated in the past, and are completely capable of today.    The lyrics are good, and Brandon's voice is great as usual, but there doesn't seem to be as much musical depth to the songs as in the past.  To be fair this album seems to be influenced by a lot of different sounds including Kings of Leon (Theives could easily be on one of their albums), and some 70's era Elton John style ballads.  But the title track sounds like it's from a Disney film.  It reminded me of something that could've been in Aladdin or Hercules, not because it was bad, but because the music was that simplistic.  What bugs me about this album the most is it's uninspired and forgettable.  This is yet another example of a great, creative, and unique band putting out average music that could be done by any band.    What I would really like to see Incubus try sometime is to go back to the mindset that lead to the Morning View album, where they essentially went someplace and just vibed out for awhile.  I'd love to hear them put together an album that has more Asian sounds similar to the last song on Morning View, \\"Aqueous Transmissions.\\"  I think that would've made a much better \\"mellow\\" Incubus album if they had at least tried to infuse some sort of unique sound into their songs instead of just resting comfortably on repetitive everyday sounds and beats. There are glimmers of brilliance on this album that I think could lead to some decent sounds later on, and as much as I'd like to rate this album higher...the truth is when I asked myself \\"would I like them to make another album like this one?\\"  The answer was, absolutely not, and if they did they'd no longer be my favorite band.  So the best I can do is pass this one off as a misstep that may appeal to some, but will probably go down as one of their worst albums, and wait for the next album (which I hope comes sooner than usual).

Abrams' \\"Super 8\\" hearkens back to the days of early Steven Spielberg (who co-produced the film) - a time when summer blockbusters could appeal as much to the heart as to the eye, when a group of intrepid suburban kids could unravel mysteries and take on evil forces, when the military-industrial complex was clearly up to no good, and when tear-stained characters could look in wide-eyed, tight-shot amazement at wonders unraveling above them in the heavens.<br /><br />Set, appropriately, in 1979 - in a time before digital cameras and CGI technology, when children had to express their creativity and imagination with what was then the technological equivalent of chewing gum and baling wire - \\"Super 8\\" centers on a group of nerdish preteens who are attempting to make their own amateur zombie-splatter film for an upcoming contest. While filming one night, the kids are witness to a horrifying freight train crash, the repercussions of which go far beyond just the mass of mangled, twisted metal strewn across the area. For it seems the Air Force has taken a more than casual interest in the event, and soon strange and inexplicable occurrences begin happening in town.<br /><br />The focal point of the story is Joe Lamb (Joel Courtney), a young boy whose mother has just been killed in a tragic factory accident, and whose grieving father (Kyle Chandler from TV's incomparable \\"Friday Night Lights\\") serves as the town's deputy. Joe also has a crush on the pretty and alluring Alice Dainard (Elle Fanning), who has been brought into the boys' film project at the last moment.<br /><br />The similarities between \\"Super 8\\" and any number of Spielberg - or at least Spielbergian - films (most notably, of course, \\"E.T.\\") are both so numerous and so obvious that they really don't bear repeating here. Yet, while I admire much of what Abrams is trying to do in this movie, so much of it falls flat that I really can't recommend it in the final analysis. What's actually missing is that special Spielberg touch, that almost Quixotic visionary conviction that could turn even a simple work like \\"E.T.\\" into a timeless classic. One never questions Spielberg's belief in his material, but I'm not sure one can quite make the same claim about Abrams, at least not in this particular instance. Abrams' story lacks the streamlined clarity of an \\"E.T.\\" and, by extension, the emotional resonance that comes along with it. \\"Super 8\\" seems to be going off in so many different directions at once that it winds up going nowhere. B004WKOCP8|3.0|They may be using their \\"classic\\" logo -- the one associated with alt-rock/nu-era favorites such as \\"S.C.I.E.N.C.E.\\" and \\"Make Yourself\\" -- in conjunction with their seventh album, \\"If Not Now, When?\\" but don't expect much of a return to form for Incubus. With nearly half a decade gone by since the release of their last studio album, \\"Light Grenades,\\" there's a noticeable evolution in sound, for better or worse.<br /><br />While Incubus have always strived to re-invent themselves with the release of each album, the shift in sound and style on \\"If Not Now, When?\\" may be just a bit too jarring for even the most hardcore of hardcore fans. Gone are the noodlings of guitarist Mike Einziger (which were at the forefront of the band's previously most polarizing \\"A Crow Left of the Murder\\") and the emotive and often empowering words of frontman Brandon Boyd. What has emerged and what defines Incubus circa 2011 is, for lack of a better term, a blend of adult contemporary and alt-rock. Quite frankly, it's a surprisingly middle-of-the-road sound for a band who has, time and time again, proven themselves capable of tackling several genres in a unique and creative fashion.<br /><br />On one hand, it's refreshing to hear a mainstream band refusing to cater to one particular demographic while pushing themselves in different directions, but unfortunately the creativity and unique compositions that had previously defined the group are all but vanished, save for a few savory numbers such as \\"Adolescents\\" and \\"In The Company of Wolves,\\" which are hardly vintage Incubus but bright spots nevertheless. What really rubs the salt in the wound though is the performance by Boyd, whose delivery on the likes of \\"Promises, Promises\\" and \\"Isadore\\" will likely irritate the average listener's gag reflex, unless, of course, you're a twenty-something female who imagines herself being seduced by the band. Boyd's voice still sounds just as good as ever, but his lyrics hit an all-time low and drag down the potential of quite a few decent tracks.<br /><br />It'd be unfair to outright call this a bad album. A more appropriate adjective to assign to the experience of listening to \\"If Not Now, When?\\" would be \\"underwhelming.\\" While there's a handful of standouts (mostly at the tail-end of the record) the album is mostly cluttered with forgettable throwaways that sound like outtakes from \\"Light Grenades\\" and \\"A Crow Left of the Murder\\" that deserved to be left on the cutting room floor. It's hard not to judge this body of work without considering the gems that precede it, but it's blatantly obvious that \\"If Not Now, When?\\" is a failed experiment of sorts and in no way, shape or form lives up to the legacy of the band that created it. All we can do now is sit and wait, hoping the band will come back sooner than later with something that washes the bad taste of this album out of our mouths. I wish Setterfield wrote more books since I think this is a great debut novel with plenty of room to grow and develop.  More modern gothic novels please!

B0050BEM1M|4.0|I'm kind of new into the whole Mike Leigh film-fan thing.  I've seen a few of his films, but I actually really need to get into his 90's work, which is where a lot of my friends say his real gems are.  That being said, I really do like what I've seen so far.  Even films that initially didn't strike me (like `Happy-Go-Lucky') have intrigued me all the more as time as passed; and there really is no denying that `Vera Drake' is a modern masterpiece.<br /><br />So that brings me to `Another Year', a film that seemed rather dull and bland to me (from an outsider's point of view) that wound up being anything but.<br /><br />The film revolves around an aged couple, Tom and Gerri, who live in England.  They have an eclectic group of friends, namely the middle-aged Mary.  Mary is desperate for attention, in and out of bad relationships and determined to find Mr. Right, but Mr. Right is elusive and her personality (possessive, clingy, frantic, pathetic) is a deterrent, especially when her obsession with age and feeling or appearing younger than the lines on her face would suggest cause her to seek out the affections of inappropriate suitors, namely Tom and Gerri's son Joe.  Spanning over the course of a year, the aptly named `Another Year' takes the time to expose the frailties in friendships as well as the lasting bond formed by those who share something special.  As the characters are introduced and incorporated into the fabric of Tom and Gerri's lives, we can see how the dynamic works between everyone.  Each character has their own identity, all but Tom and Gerri who seem to be mere props to `tie everyone together'.  That is probably my one quibble with this film.  I would have liked to see the two central characters feel a little more alive, especially Tom, but at the end of the day their reflective and observational personalities allowed the more aggressive characters to shine, so maybe it was for the best.<br /><br />We can't all be scene stealers.<br /><br />The film is broken down into four sections (Spring, Summer, Autumn, Winter), and those sections mainly consist of one elongated scene each, thus playing out much like a play would.  I actually adore this structure because it allows the dialog and character interaction to take center stage and really unfold beautifully, giving the characters rich development and persona's all their own that mesh and conflict and cause that feeling of awkward, uneasiness that lets the audience know that what is happening is `real', as in `authentic'.  And that is really one of the best assets this film has in its arsenal; it feels real.  The acting is all splendid, especially Lesley Manville who nails every ounce of Mary's emotional complexity.  She creates a character that is equal parts adorable and repulsive.  You can understand why Tom and Gerri love her, but you can also understand why she grates their nerves so excessively, and it is very hard to construct a character that obnoxious that doesn't make the audience long for their departure.  In fact, I kept hoping she'd come back the moment she faded from view.<br /><br />Parts of `Another Year' could have been cleaned up, especially in the first section, which took some time to pick up, but overall I really enjoyed what Leigh did with his characters here.  This is the type of film that demands subsequent viewings, for once is certainly not enough to fully `get' everything that Leigh is delivering.(Spring, Summer, Autumn, Winter), and those sections mainly consist of one elongated scene each, thus playing out much like a play would.  I actually adore this structure because it allows the dialog and character interaction to take center stage and really unfold beautifully, giving the characters rich development and persona's all their own that mesh and conflict and cause that feeling of awkward, uneasiness that lets the audience know that what is happening is `real', as in `authentic'.  And that is really one of the best assets this film has in its arsenal; it feels real.  The acting is all splendid, especially Lesley Manville who nails every ounce of Mary's emotional complexity.  She creates a character that is equal parts adorable and repulsive.  You can understand why Tom and Gerri love her, but you can also understand why she grates their nerves so excessively, and it is very hard to construct a character that obnoxious that doesn't make the audience long for their departure.  In fact, I kept hoping she'd come back the moment she faded from view.    Parts of `Another Year' could have been cleaned up, especially in the first section, which took some time to pick up, but overall I really enjoyed what Leigh did with his characters here.  This is the type of film that demands subsequent viewings, for once is certainly not enough to fully `get' everything that Leigh is delivering.

B0000WN0ZK|5.0|Where does your imagination lead you? Blowup is a 1966 film directed by Michelangelo Antonioni, his first English-language film. Right off this is arguably a pretentious filmmakers dream come true. The level of style over substance in this film borders on comical yet somehow it all works well. It is true that a person could make the argument that this film is a major in depth critique on the roots of imagination. On the other hand you could also make the argument that this film is merely a romp that mixes attractive female models with illegal substances.  The hero in this film is a glamorous photographer Thomas (David Hemmings). He is really a pompous jerk but the kind people can relate to. Thomas exudes relatability because he is alone. This is a story about alienation. In that sense the film is timeless. Blow Up takes place in London in the mid 60s. It is a time capsule of style. The photographer is cruising around the city and stops to take a few pictures of a random couple in the park. It turns out that the pictures he took miraculously reveal that there was a murder in progress. This leads to him having a suggestive and revealing attractive woman in his apartment. One thing leads to another. If you want to be happy then be happy. If you want to be sad so be it. The real beauty of this interesting film is that it really leaves it up to you. You might even dislike Blowup but good luck looking away or turning it off. You never truly know what will happen next. The soundtrack was scored by Herbie Hancock. There is also a particularly unique performance by the Yardbirds where everyone in the audience is really just to cool to care. Sound familiar? Borrow this one from the library. It will ultimately remind you of a time that is very similar to now. That is the power of Blowup.

068485743X|5.0|Noah Lukeman's The First Five Pages provides excellent advice for beginning writers, so this book is not for more experienced writers.<br /><br />What I found interesting is that Lukeman addresses the questions of many who attend writers conferences. They ask editors and agents if formatting, spelling, grammar, and noun-verb dense writing are important. The editors and agents politely explain that they are, and I suspect they would just as soon roll their eyes.<br /><br />Moreover, I've read manuscripts that contain such errors, and I wonder if the writers think their ideas or their plots are more important than manuscript etiquette, so I know first-hand that Lukeman addresses problems that shouldn't be.<br /><br />Lukeman does not promise publishing success, nor does he offer advice regarding characterization or plot development that other books do. He simply points out reasons why many manuscripts are rejected. Since publishing houses employ fewer copy editors and proofreaders, Lukeman's advice is particularly appropriate.<br /><br />I believe that The First Five Pages is more useful than many creative writing textbooks that are replete with artificial exercises. Lukeman provides room for writers to write their own stories.

1451629184|5.0|Jaycee Lee Dugard's \\"a stolen life\\" rings with the kind of honesty and truth that make it difficult to describe without resorting to clichs.     Since she was abducted on June 10, 1991 by previously convicted sex offender Phillip Garrido, and finally freed on August 26, 2009, Ms. Dugard has produced a searing journal of her unique experience. In between those two dates, she was forced to bear the daughters Garrido fathered, and held prisoner for longer than probably any other child who has ultimately escaped her kidnappers. Consequently, this story really has the feel of a secret unearthed, and a victory against all odds, when even many of those investigating her case must have wondered if the girl who disappeared so long ago would ever be seen again. As a spokesman for the National Center for Missing and Exploited Children said, \\"She's alive, and she becomes a symbol of hope for so many searching families.\\"    \\"A stolen life\\" is a harrowing book, but Ms. Dugard makes it bearable and instructive. Part of her considerable skill as a writer comes down to simply knowing what she wants to say in the first place. And while much of her tale is confusing, non-linear, and sometimes even contradictory, in some ways these qualities are more suited to her narrative. As she notes at the beginning, the jumbled nature of her memories and thoughts reflects the disorientation of being held captive for 18 years by a cruel and controlling pedophile.    When Ms. Dugard talks about the sexual abuse she suffered at the hands of Garrido, she is candid but dignified, never shying away from detail where required for context but avoiding any appearance of sensationalism throughout the text. Her bravery in recounting the violent \\"runs,\\" during which Garrido would subject her to extended bouts of drug-fueled sexual molestation, is both heartbreaking in its explicit particulars and a model of decorum all at the same time.    The book has a few flaws, but those are mostly technical. Ms. Dugard commits several unforced grammatical and spelling errors, but even through the occasional tautology (\\"two a.m. in the morning,\\" on p. 111, for instance) she describes her ordeal with clarity, precision and perspective.    And yet Ms. Dugard is unpredictable in her views. Her personality is full of paradoxes. She doesn't take drugs. She doesn't trust cops. She repeatedly calls herself a coward but endures unimaginable pain with a resigned stoicism that would make any soldier proud. She doesn't consider herself religious but wonders about the role that souls play in human relationships. She is understandably angry about the horrors visited upon her, and how they were allowed to happen, but she does not wallow in self-pity or long for vengeance beyond the hope that justice will prevail.    Some reviewers have complained that too much of \\"a stolen life\\" involves accounts of the various cats (and the rare dog or two) the author has known. However, this criticism seems misguided when you realize that - apart from being an animal lover even before she was snatched away from her family - caring for other creatures was an important coping mechanism that helped keep her sane when she had to deal with a degree of loneliness that would have driven a weaker person crazy. Indeed. You don't have to agree with everything Ms. Dugard says to recognize this is memoir of uncommon power and exceptional value that readers will be discussing - and benefiting from - for many years.    And that brings up the need for one last word to the Internet tough guys who blather that Ms. Dugard should have done more to escape, fight or struggle against her tormentors. Take this challenge. Listen to this lady's side of the story and try to explain how you could do better than her, especially when she was a terrified 11-year-old child in a nightmare situation, and then an adult who had to think of how to protect her children, too. Also remember that, for most of her time she was missing, Ms. Dugard was extremely isolated from the rest of the world. As the days turned into weeks, and the months into decades, news of outside events seemed not to penetrate the \\"backyard\\" compound where she did her best to get by and care for her daughters. And if she did hear of the harsh reality that existed outside, the attacks on 9/11, for example, who could blame her if she was scared of the other, unknown dangers lurking elsewhere? Maybe it was easier to dream of a better future and wait until she had the opportunity to ensure that her release would mean safety at last.      Whenever any critic reviews an author's creation, one of the questions he or she must ask is, \\"Was there anything to be learned from this work?\\"    With \\"a stolen life,\\" the answer is a resounding yes. For anybody who has ever felt insignificant, overlooked, or powerless to break out of a trap or find his or her own brand of happiness, that is, most of us, Ms. Dugard's courage is a lesson in perseverance.    In the end, the impression one most gets from reading \\"a stolen life\\" is that of a strong woman who is far more of a survivor than a victim. I can't wait for Ms. Dugard's next book. May she live long and prosper.

Worthwhile to know what everyone is talking about - and for some pretty amusing stories - but not recommended as a parenting how-to book.

B000040OBS|5.0|Suicide debut album is perhaps one of the very unique albums to ever be made. Historically, it arrived after the sheer experimental music landscape of the late 60s' and before the new wave/synth pop explosion of the 80s'. Culturally it lies somewhere between the aggression of low class youth and the iconoclasm of the artistic cliques; and those are just remotely similar to the output of Vega & Rev. Musically its somewhere in the middle of a triangle of musical genres; on one angle lies Electronica, on the other one Pop and on the other one Rock (punk rock most likely).<br /><br />Yet despite being an utterly unique album, a variety of musical genres that dominated the next decade, from post punk to dance, owed to Suicide for being the proto-pioneers of these trends. And it is evident by listening to the material that so many genres come to mind. True, it is an experimental album on the surface, but only in a vague sense of the word; its not musically a bin of noise or a rhythmically irregular work as those of their contemporaries Throbbing Gristle. It does have experimental elements; the vocal range from whispery to cacophonies of screams, as well as the minimalist melodies of the cheap synthesizers. Outside those elements the songs are full fledged compositions just as mainstream music, but heavily processed as into raw synthesized melodies and eery vocal deliveries. This is what makes the individual songs, from the satirical \\"Ghost Rider\\" to the ominous pseudo-harmonies of \\"Keep Your Dreams\\", work as single, powerful works of their own.<br /><br />Suicide was a product of many sorts and their intense debut album shows it. Influences as the likes of Krautrock contemporaries such as Can, and hard edge proto punk figures such as The Velvet Underground and The Stooges come to mind. This only adds to their cultural mystique; the duo were aggressive personalities far and out from the status quo, culturally placed between American outcasts and underground, Europe-flavored artistic mentalities, and this can be appreciated in the lyrical content of the album. Despite being so different and unique, the band's first two albums spawned a generation of fashionable cliques in music over the next decade.
