1594202842|3.0|Part memoir, part dyspeptic Dr. Spock, Amy Chua sparked quite a \\"national conversation\\" with this book and its pre-publication PR.  Having the right cultural background and liberal creds, Ms. Chua is able to engender discussion where others would have found opprobrium.  That being said, the book would likely have been less successful if not for the resonance of some of her points (irrespective of her child-rearing methods).  The one constant throughout the book is her criticism of American (Occidental) parenting.  I'm using Occidental in this sense to mean American parents who are not of Asian, especially Far-East Asian, descent.  One of the initial issues one encounters with the book is her lack of specificity.  Does she really mean that all Asians parent in this way?  Does this style of parenting cross geographical, cultural and class boundaries?  This problem is compounded by side-stepping the nature/nurture question early on in the book.  Someone in her social circle who wants to stay there simply must say, albeit briefly, that tiger mothers can be of any lineage and the results will be equally fantastic.  Of course, one gets the sense that she doesn't entirely accept the blank slate hypothesis that represents conventional wisdom among her peers.  Towards the end of the book, she speaks of several frustrating weeks attempting to train a seemingly obstinate dog.  After doing some research and finding the canine to not be among the more intelligent breeds, she relaxes and accepts the dog for what it is.  Although she claims the dog is not a metaphor, it's difficult to explain the inclusion of the dog training vignette if it isn't.  The fact is that Chua's father is a UC Berkley Electrical Engineering Professor and she's a graduate of both Harvard College and Harvard Law School.  Her husband is Jewish and a graduate of Princeton University and Harvard Law School.  Is it possible that genetics could play a role in the success of her daughters?  She certainly won't admit it, but I suspect she realizes that there's a strong likelihood of its possibility.  There are some other points that it would have been helpful for her to address.  For example, the occidental parenting that she loathes is of a more recent vintage.  Helicopter parenting, the belief that one's child is an excellent student and that a lesser grade is the teacher's failing, etc., are additional gifts bequeathed to us by the baby boom generation.  Just a couple of generations ago, many parents would have been more likely to push their children to work harder and study more in the face of lagging grades.  This isn't to say that Ms. Chua's strict standards were ever the norm in the U.S., but strong, responsible families were certainly in greater supply.  The author also fails to deal adequately with the negative aspects of her style of parenting.  Clearly it doesn't work on all Asian children; her father and youngest daughter are testaments to this.  More importantly, are there externalities associated with raising children in such a strict, regimented way?  Mainland China has been locked in Communist rule for well over 60 years.  Tens of millions have lost their lives at the hands of, or as a result of, the government.  Yet throughout it all, very little in the way of rebellion has occurred on Chinese soil (Tiananmen Square protests being a notable exception).  Perhaps the rigidity she advocates produces well-mannered, well-educated conformists.  I don't know and she never explores the idea.  Finally, the impact of her parenting on her husband is dealt with only infrequently and obliquely.  Her husband does not seem to be fully on board with all of her methods, but they keep their disagreements hidden from their children.  While a united front may spare the children additional strife and conflicting signals, it doesn't resolve the underlying divergence of opinion that exists between the married couple.  I certainly won't defend the sorry state that parenting has devolved into in the United States over the course of the past 45+ years, but as I think Ms. Chua came to realize, her ways aren't always effective either.  Battle Hymn is an interesting read and a quick one at that.  I do think it suffers from, among other things, a lack of focus.  I'm not entirely sure of the author's intent for the book: memoir, prescription, proscription, income source for music supplies???  If you're looking for a positive lesson in how to raise your child, this isn't the book for you. Surprisingly, despite some of her negativity and tone, you come away less with a poor impression of the author than with a desire to discuss/debate what you've read and there's something to be said for that.

B00005MNP7|5.0|When you hear 1978's `[[ASIN:B00005MNP8 Parallel Lines]]', it is hard to believe Blondie could top it. But a year later, they did. From the energetic drum rolls and simple opening new wave lead guitar strains of `Dreaming' to the closing bars of `Living in the Real World' twelve songs later, the drive and energy of Blondie's crowning achievement, `Eat to the Beat', never lets up.<br /><br />Producer Mike Chapman seems to have taken a different approach on this follow up to his massively successful first album with the band. `Eat to the Beat' has a sense of abandon all the way through. It's completely irresistible pop new wave onslaught almost manages to make `Parallel Lines' sound calculated and lumbering by comparison (which, of course, it definitely isn't!). It is a culmination of everything they accomplished on that record - all the influences and ideas that had characterized their style on their first three albums ... amped!<br /><br />Everyone is in top form on `Eat to the Beat', from musicianship to vocal performance to song-writing (of which everyone but drummer Clem Burke contributes) and, of course, the stellar thumping-60s-girl-group-crossed-with-new-wave production. Burke might not be a writer on this one, but his drumming is the centrepiece around which the entire record is built. On `Eat to the Beat', he comes into his own.<br /><br />On this fourth outing, guitarist Chris Stein and keyboardist Jimmy Destri crystalize their roles as the two major stylists in the band. When you think of the overall sound of `Eat to the Beat' it is their writing that defines it (often with Deborah Harry, who co-wrote three quarters of the tracks). Stein is behind the 60s pop rock of `Dreaming', the rhythmic funk rock onslaught of `The Hardest Part', the reggae of `Die Young, Stay Pretty' and the wafting slow melodrama of `Shayla'. At the same time, Destri is responsible for the gorgeous strains of `Accidents Never Happen', the delirious 60s girl group pop of `Slow Motion', the new wave disco explosion of the timeless, career-defining `Atomic' and the closing New York punk of `Living in the Real World'. But it is the incredible departures, co-written by Harry with other band members that make the whole package of `Eat to the Beat' so memorable. With guitarist Nigel Harrison, she crafted the anthemic `Union City Blue' and with bassist, Frank Infante, the hysterically tortured hard rocker, `Victor'.<br /><br />`Eat to the Beat' is so fantastic, it leaves you breathless. It is the band's crowning achievement, bringing everything together in one prefect 45 minute blast. It is a shame it has had to spend its life in the shadow of its predecessor - but that makes it such an incredible discovery for the uninitiated.<br /><br />The 2001 remaster is superb. But as usual, the bonus tracks are unnecessary, particularly the abysmal live cover of David Bowie's `Heroes'. Though, `Seven Rooms of Gloom' would have been right at home on the original set! A MUST OWN record!plosion of the timeless, career-defining `Atomic' and the closing New York punk of `Living in the Real World'. With guitarist Nigel Harrison, she crafted the anthemic `Union City Blue' and with bassist, Frank Infante, the hysterically tortured hard rocker, `Victor'.     `Eat to the Beat' is so fantastic, it leaves you breathless. 0553801473|2.0|I was a huge fan of the first four books. The grim realism, the details, the mix of flawed characters and their flawed decisions making such a huge mess, the crafty storytelling - all of it set this series miles and miles above much of the fantasy out there.<br /><br />Something went wrong here though. The first 350 pages of this book are painfully boring, with the exception of Bran and \\"Reek.\\" We have travelogues, endless tangents about irrelevant people and places, far too much exposition, and chapters that could have been cut. I am all for atmosphere and immersion into a fictional world, but there's a point where the story gets buried under it and it becomes self-indulgent. The book picks up a bit somewhere between a third and halfway in, once the plot in the North gets going, but overall it's still bloated and riddled with POV characters I'm not entirely sure needed to be POV characters.<br /><br />(Warning: Some spoilers ahead)<br /><br />Dany, Jon, and Tyrion are the least interesting of all the POVs, and sadly they take up most of the book. Dany is shown to be an inept ruler, emotional and slightly crazy, and after pretty much showing us the same thing in different ways from his only other female rulers, Cersie and Lysa Tully, he seems to be endorsing the notion that women (particularly mothers) are unfit to rule. Men have honor, women have emotions, seems to be his idea when comparing Dany with equally well-intentioned but unsuccessful male rulers such as Ned and Jon. Tyrion's arc is meandering and ridiculous, and goes nowhere. Tyrion himself has become unsympathetic and somewhat disgusting. I am sick and tired of his sense of entitlement to the mutual love and attraction of beautiful women, and the terrible things he does to them when he doesn't get it. And this is GRRM's favorite character. Jon is the best of the three, taking on the hard task of putting aside centuries of bad blood between his people and their enemies in order to work together to save humanity from a worse enemy. He cares for others and does the best he can. Unfortunately, his arc ends in a tacky cliffhanger.<br /><br />There are other problems too besides a serious lack of entertainment value. GRRM's gotten lazy and repetitive. The same phrases are used over and over. The good old switcheroo/fake death is overused. He used it well with Bran and Rickon in the second book, and with the fake Arya, but now we have this ridiculous &lt;s>shark jump&lt;/s>\\"plot twist\\" where a long presumed characters turns up so he can... have Dany's plotline! I hope he's fake and a political pawn, but nonetheless the story arc needlessly convolutes the story. Another character gets magically switcheroo'd at the Wall, Davos gets switcheroo'd, and there's always been the possibility that Jeyne Stark (nee Westerling) has switcheroo'd herself as well.  Ashara Dayne might be alive and well too. That's too many and all of it takes away from the realism that once made the story so great.<br /><br />Speaking of the realism, which I was as thrilled to see in a fantasy series as much anyone else, I feel we've moved past accurately and unflinchingly depicting the harsh realities of a pseudo-medeival society and descended into the dark pit of TMI about the author's twisted fixations. AFFC slid down this slope too, but it's gotten worse. Rape, rape, rape, rape, rape, rape. GRRM's begun to revel in the primitive sickness of his society, and his villains, in the crudeness and grossness. Honestly, it's getting creepy. Uncomfortably so. Same with mutilation, maiming, and horrid disfigurement. The Right of the First Night has cropped up, that fantasy of all twisted males (which is certainly not to say all males). We have a stomach-turning wedding night scene, which happens to include a male character forced into a sex act with a pretty, frightened, and naked 13 year-old girl. I'm neither a prude, nor the sort who looks for reason to be offended, but I found some of the content over the line. Villains like Ramsey Bolton, and Roose in this volume, are cheap shock value at any case. I expected more from a series of this caliber than the standard rapist/torture villain. \\"Behave or I will flay you all!\\" Come on now.<br /><br />GRRM has included his first gay male POV character, and though it's always good to see this sort of diversity, I'm wondering why he portrays sex between women as a casual diversion in order to scratch an inch, whereas male homosexuality (Loras/Renly and Connington, who loved Rhaegar) is held up in the light of great devotion and loyality. Hmm.<br /><br />All in all, I give praise for Bran, Asha, \\"Reek,\\" and Melisandre, and I may skim through subsequent volumes to see what happens to a handful of characters, but I no longer have the fierce love for the series I did before cracking this one open, and I'm very sad to have to say that.
